<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Climate change and agriculture</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector,_in_2010_(color)_png.png"><img alt="Refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="831" data-file-width="1200" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png/450px-Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png/675px-Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png/900px-Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector%2C_in_2010_%28color%29_png.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Annual_greenhouse_gas_emissions_by_sector,_in_2010_(color)_png.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Human greenhouse gas emissions by sector, in the year 2010. "AFOLU" stands for "agriculture, forestry, and other land use".</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="1448" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png/330px-Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png/495px-Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png/660px-Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Net_crops_tropicalvsworld.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph of net crop production worldwide and in selected tropical countries. Raw data from the United Nations. <a class="external autonumber" href="http://faostat3.fao.org/browse/Q/QC/E" rel="nofollow">[4]</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Climate change and agriculture</b> are interrelated processes, both of which take place on a global scale. <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Climate change</a> affects <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> in a number of ways, including through changes in <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">average temperatures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rainfall</a>, and climate <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extremes</a> (e.g., heat waves); changes in <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a> and diseases; changes in atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and ground-level <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> concentrations; changes in the <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutritional</a> quality of some foods;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science-news_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-science-news-1">[1]</a></sup> and changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_sea_level_rise" title="Current sea level rise">sea level</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climate change is already affecting agriculture, with effects unevenly distributed across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_summary_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-porter_summary-3">[3]</a></sup> Future climate change will likely negatively affect <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop production</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_latitude" title="Low latitude">low latitude</a> countries, while effects in northern <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a> may be positive or negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_summary_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-porter_summary-3">[3]</a></sup> Climate change will probably increase the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_insecurity" title="Food insecurity">food insecurity</a> for some vulnerable groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Animal agriculture is also responsible for greenhouse gas production of CO<sub>2</sub> and a percentage of the world's methane, and future land infertility, and the displacement of local species.
</p><p>Agriculture contributes to climate change both by <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> emissions of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> and by the conversion of non-agricultural land such as <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forests</a> into agricultural land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Agriculture, forestry and land-use change contributed around 20 to 25% to global annual emissions in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>A range of policies can reduce the risk of negative climate change impacts on agriculture<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and greenhouse gas emissions from the agriculture sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_climate_change_on_agriculture">Impact of climate change on agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Impact of climate change on agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_(ARS_USDA).png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="691" data-file-width="1765" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png/450px-Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png/675px-Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png/900px-Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_%28ARS_USDA%29.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Corn_and_soybean_temperature_response_(ARS_USDA).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>For each plant variety, there is an optimal temperature for vegetative growth, with growth dropping off as temperatures increase or decrease. Similarly, there is a range of temperatures at which a plant will produce seed. Outside of this range, the plant will not reproduce. As the graphs show, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn" title="Corn">corn</a> will fail to reproduce at temperatures above 95 °F (35 °C) and <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a> above 102 °F (38.8 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Despite technological advances, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crop_breeding" title="Crop breeding">improved varieties</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GMO" title="GMO">genetically modified organisms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> systems, weather is still a key factor in agricultural productivity, as well as <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> properties and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">natural communities</a>. The effect of climate on agriculture is related to variabilities in local climates rather than in global climate patterns. The Earth's average surface temperature has increased by 1.5 °F (0.83 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C2%B0C" title="°C">°C</a>) since 1880. Consequently, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agronomist" title="Agronomist">agronomists</a> consider any assessment has to be individually consider each <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">local area</a>.
</p><p>On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_economics" title="Agricultural economics">agricultural trade</a> has grown in recent years, and now provides significant amounts of food, on a national level to major importing countries, as well as comfortable <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">income</a> to exporting ones. The international aspect of trade and security in terms of food implies the need to also consider the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of climate change</a> on a global scale.
</p><p>A 2008 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> suggested that, due to climate change, "southern Africa could lose more than 30% of its main crop, maize, by 2030. In South Asia losses of many regional staples, such as rice, millet and maize could top 10%".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> (IPCC) has produced several reports that have assessed the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_literature" title="Scientific literature">scientific literature</a> on climate change. The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Third_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Third Assessment Report">IPCC Third Assessment Report</a>, published in 2001, concluded that the poorest countries would be hardest hit, with reductions in crop yields in most tropical and sub-tropical regions due to decreased water availability, and new or changed insect pest incidence. In Africa and Latin America many rainfed crops are near their maximum temperature tolerance, so that yields are likely to fall sharply for even small climate changes; falls in agricultural productivity of up to 30% over the 21st century are projected. Marine life and the fishing industry will also be severely affected in some places.
</p><p>Climate change induced by increasing <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> is likely to affect crops differently from region to region. For example, average crop yield is expected to drop down to 50% in Pakistan according to the <a href="/wiki/Met_Office" title="Met Office">Met Office</a> scenario whereas corn production in Europe is expected to grow up to 25% in optimum <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrologic" title="Hydrologic">hydrologic</a> conditions.
</p><p>More favourable effects on yield tend to depend to a large extent on realization of the potentially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beneficial_effects_of_global_warming" title="Beneficial effects of global warming">beneficial effects of carbon dioxide on crop growth</a> and increase of efficiency in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_use" title="Water use">water use</a>. Decrease in potential yields is likely to be caused by shortening of the growing period, decrease in water availability and poor <a href="/wiki/Vernalization" title="Vernalization">vernalization</a>.
</p><p>In the long run, the climatic change could affect agriculture in several ways :
</p>
<ul><li><i>productivity</i>, in terms of <a href="/wiki/Quantity" title="Quantity">quantity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quality_(business)" title="Quality (business)">quality</a> of crops</li>
<li><i>agricultural practices</i>, through changes of water use (irrigation) and agricultural inputs such as <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a></li>
<li><i>environmental effects</i>, in particular in relation of frequency and intensity of soil <a href="/wiki/Drainage" title="Drainage">drainage</a> (leading to nitrogen leaching), <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, reduction of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">crop diversity</a></li>
<li><i>rural space</i>, through the loss and gain of cultivated lands, land <a href="/wiki/Speculation" title="Speculation">speculation</a>, land renunciation, and hydraulic amenities.</li>
<li><i>adaptation</i>, organisms may become more or less competitive, as well as humans may develop urgency to develop more competitive organisms, such as flood resistant or <a href="/wiki/Crop_tolerance_to_seawater" title="Crop tolerance to seawater">salt resistant</a> varieties of rice.</li></ul>
<p>They are large uncertainties to uncover, particularly because there is lack of information on many specific local regions, and include the uncertainties on magnitude of climate change, the effects of technological changes on productivity, global food demands, and the numerous possibilities of adaptation.
</p><p>Most agronomists believe that agricultural production will be mostly affected by the severity and pace of climate change, not so much by gradual trends in climate. If change is gradual, there may be enough time for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biota_(ecology)" title="Biota (ecology)">biota</a> adjustment. Rapid climate change, however, could harm agriculture in many countries, especially those that are already suffering from rather poor soil and climate conditions, because there is less time for optimum <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> and adaption.
</p><p>But much remains unknown about exactly how <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> may affect farming and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>, in part because the role of farmer behaviour is poorly captured by crop-climate models.  For instance, Evan Fraser, a geographer at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Guelph" title="University of Guelph">University of Guelph</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, has conducted a number of studies that show that the socio-economic context of farming may play a huge role in determining whether a <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> has a major, or an insignificant impact on crop production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simelton,_E._2009_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simelton,_E._2009-16">[16]</a></sup> In some cases, it seems that even minor droughts have big impacts on food security (such as what happened in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> in the early 1980s where a minor drought triggered a massive <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>), versus cases where even relatively large weather-related problems were adapted to without much hardship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Evan Fraser combines socio-economic models along with climatic models to identify “vulnerability hotspots”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simelton,_E._2009_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Simelton,_E._2009-16">[16]</a></sup> One such study has identified <a href="/wiki/Corn_production_in_the_United_States" title="Corn production in the United States">US maize (corn) production</a> as particularly vulnerable to climate change because it is expected to be exposed to worse droughts, but it does not have the socio-economic conditions that suggest farmers will adapt to these changing conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Other studies rely instead on projections of key agro-meteorological or agro-climate indices, such as growing season length, plant heat stress, or start of field operations, identified by land management stakeholders and that provide useful information on mechanisms driving climate change impact on agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pest_insects_and_climate_change">Pest insects and climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pest insects and climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Global warming could lead to an increase in pest insect populations, harming yields of staple crops like <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybeans</a>, and corn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Live_Science_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Live_Science-21">[21]</a></sup> While warmer temperatures create longer growing seasons, and faster growth rates for plants, it also increases the metabolic rate and number of breeding cycles of insect populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Live_Science_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Live_Science-21">[21]</a></sup> Insects that previously had only two breeding cycles per year could gain an additional cycle if warm growing seasons extend, causing a population boom. Temperate places and higher <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a> are more likely to experience a dramatic change in insect populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana%E2%80%93Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana–Champaign">University of Illinois</a> conducted studies to measure the effect of warmer temperatures on soybean plant growth and Japanese beetle populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists-23">[23]</a></sup> Warmer temperatures and elevated CO<sub>2</sub> levels were simulated for one field of soybeans, while the other was left as a control. These studies found that the soybeans with elevated CO<sub>2</sub> levels grew much faster and had higher yields, but attracted <a href="/wiki/Japanese_beetle" title="Japanese beetle">Japanese beetles</a> at a significantly higher rate than the control field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists-23">[23]</a></sup> The beetles in the field with increased CO<sub>2</sub> also laid more eggs on the soybean plants and had longer lifespans, indicating the possibility of a rapidly expanding population. DeLucia projected that if the project were to continue, the field with elevated CO<sub>2</sub> levels would eventually show lower yields than that of the control field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The increased CO<sub>2</sub> levels deactivated three genes within the soybean plant that normally create chemical defenses against pest insects. One of these defenses is a protein that blocks digestion of the soy leaves in insects. Since this gene was deactivated, the beetles were able to digest a much higher amount of plant matter than the beetles in the control field. This led to the observed longer lifespans and higher egg-laying rates in the experimental field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Union_of_Concerned_Scientists-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are a few proposed solutions to the issue of expanding pest populations. One proposed solution is to increase the number of pesticides used on future crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agadapt.ucdavis.edu_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-agadapt.ucdavis.edu-24">[24]</a></sup> This has the benefit of being relatively cost effective and simple, but may be ineffective. Many pest insects have been building up an <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_resistance" title="Pesticide resistance">immunity</a> to these pesticides. Another proposed solution is to utilize <a href="/wiki/Biological_pest_control" title="Biological pest control">biological control agents</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agadapt.ucdavis.edu_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-agadapt.ucdavis.edu-24">[24]</a></sup> This includes things like planting rows of native vegetation in between rows of crops. This solution is beneficial in its overall environmental impact. Not only are more native plants getting planted, but pest insects are no longer building up an immunity to pesticides. However, planting additional native plants requires more room, which destroys additional acres of public land. The cost is also much higher than simply using pesticides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_diseases_and_climate_change">Plant diseases and climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Plant diseases and climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although research is limited, research has shown that climate change may alter the developmental stages of pathogens that can affect crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup> The biggest consequence of climate change on the dispersal of pathogens is that the geographical distribution of hosts and pathogens could shift, which would result in more crop losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup> This could affect competition and recovery from disturbances of plants. It has been predicted that the effect of climate change will add a level of complexity to figuring out how to maintain sustainable agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Observed_impacts">Observed impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Observed impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Effects of regional climate change on agriculture have been limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosen_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-rosen-27">[27]</a></sup> Changes in crop <a href="/wiki/Phenology" title="Phenology">phenology</a> provide important evidence of the response to recent regional climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Phenology is the study of natural phenomena that recur periodically, and how these phenomena relate to climate and seasonal changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> A significant advance in phenology has been observed for agriculture and forestry in large parts of the Northern Hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosen_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-rosen-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Droughts have been occurring more frequently because of global warming and they are expected to become more frequent and intense in Africa, southern Europe, the Middle East, most of the Americas, Australia, and Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Their impacts are aggravated because of increased water demand, population growth, urban expansion, and environmental protection efforts in many areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Droughts result in crop failures and the loss of pasture grazing land for livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg/170px-Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg/255px-Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg/340px-Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Banana_farm_Chinawal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Banana farm at <a href="/wiki/Chinawal" title="Chinawal">Chinawal</a> village in <a href="/wiki/Jalgaon_district" title="Jalgaon district">Jalgaon district, India</a></div></div></div>
<p>As of the decade starting in 2010, many hot countries have thriving agricultural sectors.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Jalgaon_district" title="Jalgaon district">Jalgaon district, India</a>, has an average temperature which ranges from 20.2 °C in December to 29.8 °C in May, and an average precipitation of 750 mm/year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> It produces bananas at a rate that would make it the world's seventh-largest banana producer if it were a country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the period 1990-2012, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> had an average temperature which ranged from a low of 24.9 °C in January to a high of 30.4 °C in April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (FAO), <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> is by far the <a href="/wiki/Yam_production_in_Nigeria" title="Yam production in Nigeria">world's largest producer of yams</a>, producing over 38 million tonnes in 2012. The second through 8th largest yam producers were all nearby African countries, with the largest non-African producer, <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>, producing less than 1% of Nigerian production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, according to the FAO, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> were by far the world's leading producers of <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a>, with a combined production of over 1 billion tonnes, or over half of worldwide production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAOSTAT_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the summer of 2018, heat waves probably linked to climate change cause much lower than average yield in many parts of the world, especially in Europe. Depending on conditions during August, more crop failures could rise global food prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-This_Summer’s_Heat_Waves_Could_Be_the_Strongest_Climate_Signal_Yet_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-This_Summer’s_Heat_Waves_Could_Be_the_Strongest_Climate_Signal_Yet-38">[38]</a></sup>  losses are compared to those of 1945, the worst harvest in memory.  last year was the third time in four years that global wheat, rice and maize production failed to meet demand, forcing governments and food companies to release stocks from storage. India last week released 50% of its food stocks. Lester Brown, the head of Worldwatch, an independent research organisation, predicted thatfood  prices will rise in the next few months.
</p><p>Overall food shortages are not expected this year. But, for prevent hunger, instability, new waves of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_refugee" title="Climate refugee">Climate refugees</a>  international help to countries who will luck the money to buy enough food and stopping conflicts will be needed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heatwave_devastates_Europe's_crops_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heatwave_devastates_Europe's_crops-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conference_Fortieth_Session_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conference_Fortieth_Session-40">[40]</a></sup>(see also <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_adaptation" title="Climate change adaptation">Climate change adaptation</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections">Projections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Projections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As part of the IPCC's <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">Fourth Assessment Report</a>, Schneider <i>et al.</i> (2007) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_climate_model#Projections_of_future_climate_change" title="Global climate model">projected</a> the potential future effects of climate change on agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tab191_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-tab191-41">[41]</a></sup> With low to medium confidence, they concluded that for about a 1 to 3 °C global mean temperature increase (by 2100, relative to the 1990–2000 average level) there would be productivity decreases for some cereals in low latitudes, and productivity increases in high latitudes. In the IPCC Fourth Assessment Report, "low confidence" means that a particular finding has about a 2 out of 10 chance of being correct, based on expert judgement. "Medium confidence" has about a 5 out of 10 chance of being correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parry_uncertainty_assessment_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-parry_uncertainty_assessment-42">[42]</a></sup> Over the same time period, with medium confidence, global production potential was projected to:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tab191_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-tab191-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>increase up to around 3 °C,</li>
<li>very likely decrease above about 3 °C.</li></ul>
<p>Most of the studies on global agriculture assessed by Schneider <i>et al.</i> (2007) had not incorporated a number of critical factors, including changes in extreme events, or the spread of pests and diseases. Studies had also not considered the development of specific practices or technologies to aid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adaptation_to_global_warming#Agricultural_production" title="Adaptation to global warming">adaptation to climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_National_Research_Council" title="US National Research Council">US National Research Council</a> (US NRC, 2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1-44">[44]</a></sup> assessed the literature on the effects of climate change on crop yields. US NRC (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_text_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_text-45">[45]</a></sup> stressed the uncertainties in their projections of changes in crop yields.
</p><p>Writing in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Climate_Change" title="Nature Climate Change">Nature Climate Change</a></i>, Matthew Smith and Samuel Myers (2018) estimated that food crops could see a reduction of <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> content in common food crops of 3 to 17%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> This is the projected result of food grown under the expected atmospheric carbon-dioxide levels of 2050. Using data from the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">UN Food and Agriculture Organization</a> as well as other public sources, the authors analyzed 225 different staple foods, such as <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a>, roots and <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<table>
<tbody><tr>
<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="694" data-file-width="1322" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png/450px-Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png/675px-Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png/900px-Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_changes_in_crop_yields_at_different_latitudes_with_global_warming.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Projected changes in crop yields at different latitudes with global warming. This graph is based on several studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1-44">[44]</a></sup></div></div></div>
</td>
<td><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png"><img alt="Refer to caption" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="689" data-file-width="1345" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png/450px-Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png/675px-Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png/900px-Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_changes_in_yields_of_selected_crops_with_global_warming.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Projected changes in yields of selected crops with global warming. This graph is based on several studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1-44">[44]</a></sup></div></div></div>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Their central estimates of changes in crop yields are shown above. Actual changes in yields may be above or below these central estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_text_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_text-45">[45]</a></sup> US NRC (2011)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-us_nrc_2011_fig_5.1-44">[44]</a></sup> also provided an estimated the "likely" range of changes in yields. "Likely" means a greater than 67% chance of being correct, based on expert judgement. The likely ranges are summarized in the image descriptions of the two graphs.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_security">Food security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Food security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The IPCC Fourth Assessment Report also describes the impact of climate change on <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-easterling_sub_saharan_africa_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-easterling_sub_saharan_africa-48">[48]</a></sup> Projections suggested that there could be large decreases in <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a> globally by 2080, compared to the (then-current) 2006 level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-easterling_food_security_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-easterling_food_security-49">[49]</a></sup> Reductions in hunger were driven by projected <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>. For reference, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> has estimated that in 2006, the number of people undernourished globally was 820 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Three <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_scenario" title="Climate change scenario">scenarios</a> <i>without</i> climate change (<a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Emissions_Scenarios" title="Special Report on Emissions Scenarios">SRES</a> A1, B1, B2) projected 100-130 million undernourished by the year 2080, while another scenario without climate change (SRES A2) projected 770 million undernourished. Based on an expert assessment of all of the evidence, these projections were thought to have about a 5-in-10 chance of being correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parry_uncertainty_assessment_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-parry_uncertainty_assessment-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>The same set of greenhouse gas and socio-economic scenarios were also used in projections that included the effects of climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-easterling_food_security_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-easterling_food_security-49">[49]</a></sup> <i>Including</i> climate change, three scenarios (SRES A1, B1, B2) projected 100-380 million undernourished by the year 2080, while another scenario with climate change (SRES A2) projected 740-1,300 million undernourished. These projections were thought to have between a 2-in-10 and 5-in-10 chance of being correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parry_uncertainty_assessment_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-parry_uncertainty_assessment-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Projections also suggested regional changes in the global distribution of hunger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-easterling_food_security_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-easterling_food_security-49">[49]</a></sup> By 2080, <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> may overtake <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> as the world's most food-insecure region. This is mainly due to projected social and economic changes, rather than climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-easterling_sub_saharan_africa_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-easterling_sub_saharan_africa-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In South America, a phenomenon known as the El Nino Oscillation Cycle, between floods and drought on the Pacific Coast has made as much as a 35% difference in Global yields of wheat and grain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Looking at the four key components of food security we can see the impact climate change has had. Food “Access to food is largely a matter of household and individual-level income and of capabilities and rights” (Wheeler et al.,2013).  Access has been affected by the thousands of crops being destroyed, how communities are dealing with climate shocks and adapting to climate change. Prices on food will rise due to the shortage of food production due to conditions not being favourable for crop production. Utilization is affected by floods and drought where water resources are contaminated, and the changing temperatures create vicious stages and phases of disease. Availability is affected by the contamination of the crops, as there will be no food process for the products of these crops as a result. Stability is affected through price ranges and future prices as some food sources are becoming scarce due to climate change, so prices will rise.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual_studies">Individual studies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Individual studies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_(Cline,_2007).png"><img alt="Refer to caption and adjacent text" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="713" data-file-width="1200" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png/450px-Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png/675px-Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png/900px-Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_%28Cline%2C_2007%29.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Projected_effect_of_climate_change_on_agricultural_productivity_for_different_regions_(Cline,_2007).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Projections by Cline (2008).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cline_projections_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-cline_projections-52">[52]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Cline (2008)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cline_projections_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-cline_projections-52">[52]</a></sup> looked at how climate change might affect agricultural productivity in the 2080s. His study assumes that no efforts are made to reduce anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions, leading to global warming of 3.3 °C above the pre-industrial level. He concluded that global agricultural productivity could be negatively affected by climate change, with the worst effects in developing countries (see graph opposite).
</p><p>Lobell <i>et al.</i> (2008a)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> assessed how climate change might affect 12 food-insecure regions in 2030. The purpose of their analysis was to assess where adaptation measures to climate change should be prioritized. They found that without sufficient adaptation measures, South Asia and South Africa would likely suffer negative impacts on several crops which are important to large food insecure human populations.
</p><p>Battisti and Naylor (2009)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-battisti_seasonal_temp_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-battisti_seasonal_temp-54">[54]</a></sup> looked at how increased seasonal temperatures might affect agricultural productivity. Projections by the IPCC suggest that with climate change, high seasonal temperatures will become widespread, with the likelihood of extreme temperatures increasing through the second half of the 21st century. Battisti and Naylor (2009)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-battisti_seasonal_temp_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-battisti_seasonal_temp-54">[54]</a></sup> concluded that such changes could have very serious effects on agriculture, particularly in the tropics. They suggest that major, near-term, investments in adaptation measures could reduce these risks.
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> merely increases the urgency of reforming trade policies to ensure that global <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> needs are met"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ictsd_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-ictsd-55">[55]</a></sup> said C. Bellmann, <a href="/wiki/International_Centre_for_Trade_and_Sustainable_Development" title="International Centre for Trade and Sustainable Development">ICTSD</a> Programmes Director.  A 2009 ICTSD-IPC study by Jodie Keane<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-study_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-study-56">[56]</a></sup> suggests that <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> could cause farm output in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> to decrease by 12% by 2080 - although in some African countries this figure could be as much as 60%, with <a href="/wiki/Agreement_on_Agriculture" title="Agreement on Agriculture">agricultural exports</a> declining by up to one fifth in others. Adapting to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> could cost the agriculture sector $14bn globally a year, the study finds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional">Regional</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Regional">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Regional_effects_of_global_warming" title="Regional effects of global warming">Regional effects of global warming</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:African_crop_production.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1048" data-file-width="1504" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/African_crop_production.png/220px-African_crop_production.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/African_crop_production.png/330px-African_crop_production.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/African_crop_production.png/440px-African_crop_production.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:African_crop_production.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>African crop production. Raw data from the United Nations.<a class="external autonumber" href="http://faostat3.fao.org/download/Q/QI/E" rel="nofollow">[5]</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Africa, IPCC (2007:13)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-57">[57]</a></sup> projected that climate variability and change would severely compromise agricultural production and access to food. This projection was assigned "high confidence."
</p><p>Africa's geography makes it particularly vulnerable to climate change, and seventy per cent of the population rely on rain-fed agriculture for their livelihoods. <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>'s official report on climate change suggests that the areas that usually get two rainfalls in the year will probably get more, and those that get only one rainy season will get far less. As of 2005, the net result was expected to be that 33% less maize—the country's staple crop—would be grown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, IPCC (2007:13)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-57">[57]</a></sup> projected that <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yields</a> could increase up to 20% by the mid-21st century. In <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central</a> and South Asia, projections suggested that yields might decrease by up to 30%, over the same time period. These projections were assigned "medium confidence." Taken together, the risk of hunger was projected to remain very high in several developing countries.
</p><p>More detailed analysis of rice yields by the <a href="/wiki/International_Rice_Research_Institute" title="International Rice Research Institute">International Rice Research Institute</a> forecast 20% reduction in yields over the region per degree Celsius of temperature rise. Rice becomes sterile if exposed to temperatures above 35 degrees for more than one hour during flowering and consequently produces no grain.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>A 2013 study by the <a href="/wiki/International_Crops_Research_Institute_for_the_Semi-Arid_Tropics" title="International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics">International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICRISAT" title="ICRISAT">ICRISAT</a>) aimed to find science-based, pro-poor approaches and techniques that would enable Asia's agricultural systems to cope with climate change, while benefitting poor and vulnerable farmers. The study's recommendations ranged from improving the use of climate information in local planning and strengthening weather-based agro-advisory services, to stimulating diversification of rural household incomes and providing incentives to farmers to adopt natural resource conservation measures to enhance forest cover, replenish groundwater and use <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> A 2014 study found that warming had increased maize yields in the <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang" title="Heilongjiang">Heilongjiang</a> region of China had increased by between 7 and 17% per decade as a result of rising temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to climate change, <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> production  will be decreased in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> by diseases, scarcity of forage, heat stress and breeding strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia_and_New_Zealand">Australia and New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Australia and New Zealand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hennessy <i>et al.</i>. (2007:509)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hennessy_australia_and_new_zealand_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-hennessy_australia_and_new_zealand-62">[62]</a></sup> assessed the literature for <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. They concluded that without further adaptation to climate change, projected impacts would likely be substantial: By 2030, production from agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a> was projected to decline over much of southern and eastern Australia, and over parts of eastern New Zealand; In New Zealand, initial benefits were projected close to major rivers and in western and southern areas. Hennessy <i>et al.</i>. (2007:509)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hennessy_australia_and_new_zealand_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-hennessy_australia_and_new_zealand-62">[62]</a></sup> placed high confidence in these projections.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>With high confidence, IPCC (2007:14)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-57">[57]</a></sup> projected that in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a>, climate change would reduce crop productivity. In <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, forest productivity was expected to decline. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>, the initial effect of climate change was projected to increase crop yields.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latin_America">Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The major agricultural products of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American" title="Latin American">Latin American</a> regions include <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> and grains, such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soybeans" title="Soybeans">soybeans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> Increased temperatures and altered hydrological cycles are predicted to translate to shorter growing seasons, overall reduced biomass production, and lower grain yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_3_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_3-65">[65]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a> alone contribute 70-90% of the total agricultural production in Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> In these and other dry regions, maize production is expected to decrease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> A study summarizing a number of impact studies of climate change on agriculture in Latin America indicated that wheat is expected to decrease in Brazil, Argentina and <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> Livestock, which is the main agricultural product for parts of Argentina, Uruguay, southern Brazil, <a href="/wiki/Venezuela" title="Venezuela">Venezuela</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a> is likely to be reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-5"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> Variability in the degree of production decrease among different regions of Latin America is likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup> For example, one 2003 study that estimated future maize production in Latin America predicted that by 2055 maize in eastern Brazil will have moderate changes while Venezuela is expected to have drastic decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Suggested potential adaptation strategies to mitigate the impacts of global warming on agriculture in Latin America include using plant breeding technologies and installing irrigation infrastructure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-6"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_justice_and_subsistence_farmers_in_Latin_America">Climate justice and subsistence farmers in Latin America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Climate justice and subsistence farmers in Latin America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Several studies that investigated the impacts of climate change on agriculture in Latin America suggest that in the poorer countries of <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>, agriculture composes the most important economic sector and the primary form of sustenance for small farmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-7"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_3_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref_3-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_4_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_4-66">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">Maize</a> is the only grain still produced as a sustenance crop on small farms in Latin American nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-8"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> Scholars argue that the projected decrease of this grain and other crops will threaten the welfare and the economic development of subsistence communities in Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_1_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-ref_1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-9"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_3_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-ref_3-65">[65]</a></sup> Food security is of particular concern to rural areas that have weak or non-existent food markets to rely on in the case food shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_5_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_5-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to scholars who considered the environmental justice implications of climate change, the expected impacts of climate change on subsistence farmers in Latin America and other developing regions are unjust for two reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_4_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref_4-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_6_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_6-68">[68]</a></sup> First, subsistence farmers in developing countries, including those in Latin America are disproportionately vulnerable to climate change<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_6_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref_6-68">[68]</a></sup> Second, these nations were the least responsible for causing the problem of anthropogenic induced climate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_6_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-ref_6-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to researchers John F. Morton and T. Roberts, disproportionate vulnerability to climate disasters is socially determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_4_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-ref_4-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_6_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-ref_6-68">[68]</a></sup> For example, socioeconomic and policy trends affecting smallholder and subsistence farmers limit their capacity to adapt to change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_4_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-ref_4-66">[66]</a></sup> According to W. Baethgen who studied the vulnerability of Latin American agriculture to climate change, a history of policies and economic dynamics has negatively impacted rural farmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-10"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> During the 1950s and through the 1980s, high inflation and appreciated real exchange rates reduced the value of agricultural exports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-11"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> As a result, farmers in Latin America received lower prices for their products compared to world market prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-12"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> Following these outcomes, Latin American policies and national crop programs aimed to stimulate agricultural intensification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-13"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup> These national crop programs benefitted larger commercial farmers more. In the 1980s and 1990s low world market prices for cereals and livestock resulted in decreased agricultural growth and increased rural poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_2_64-14"><a href="#cite_note-ref_2-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the book, Fairness in Adaptation to Climate Change, the authors describe the global injustice of climate change between the rich nations of the north, who are the most responsible for global warming and the southern poor countries and minority populations within those countries who are most vulnerable to climate change impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_6_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-ref_6-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Adaptive planning is challenged by the difficulty of predicting local scale climate change impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_4_66-4"><a href="#cite_note-ref_4-66">[66]</a></sup> An expert that considered opportunities for climate change adaptation for rural communities argues that a crucial component to adaptation should include government efforts to lessen the effects of food shortages and famines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_7_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref_7-69">[69]</a></sup> This researcher also claims that planning for equitable adaptation and agricultural sustainability will require the engagement of farmers in decision making processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref_7_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-ref_7-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A number of studies have been produced which assess the impacts of climate change on agriculture in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>. The IPCC Fourth Assessment Report of agricultural impacts in the region cites 26 different studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> With high confidence, IPCC (2007:14–15)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-ar4_summary_of_regional_impacts-57">[57]</a></sup> projected that over the first few decades of this century, moderate climate change would increase aggregate yields of rain-fed agriculture by 5–20%, but with important variability among regions. Major challenges were projected for crops that are near the warm end of their suitable range or which depend on highly utilized water resources.
</p><p>Droughts are becoming more frequent and intense in arid and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semiarid" title="Semiarid">semiarid</a> western North America as temperatures have been rising, advancing the timing and magnitude of spring snow melt ﬂoods and reducing river flow volume in summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Direct effects of climate change include increased heat and water stress, altered crop <a href="/wiki/Phenology" title="Phenology">phenology</a>, and disrupted symbiotic interactions. These effects may be exacerbated by climate changes in river ﬂow, and the combined effects are likely to reduce the abundance of native trees in favor of non-native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbaceous" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> and drought-tolerant competitors, reduce the habitat quality for many native animals, and slow litter decomposition and <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrient</a> cycling. Climate change effects on human water demand and irrigation may intensify these effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>The US Global Change Research Program (2009) assessed the literature on the impacts of climate change on agriculture in the United States, finding that many crops will benefit from increased atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations and low levels of warming, but that higher levels of warming will negatively affect growth and yields; that extreme weather events will likely reduce crop yields; that <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">diseases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insect</a> <a href="/wiki/Pest_(organism)" title="Pest (organism)">pests</a> will benefit from warming, and will require additional <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weed_control" title="Weed control">weed control</a>; and that increasing CO<sub>2</sub> concentrations will reduce the land's ability to supply adequate livestock feed, while increased heat, disease, and weather extremes will likely reduce livestock productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_regions">Polar regions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Polar regions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Anisimov <i>et al.</i>. (2007:655)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> assessed the literature for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_region" title="Polar region">polar region</a> (<a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>). With medium confidence, they concluded that the benefits of a less severe climate were dependent on local conditions. One of these benefits was judged to be increased agricultural and forestry opportunities.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported on how climate change had affected agriculture in Iceland. Rising temperatures had made the widespread sowing of <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> possible, which had been untenable twenty years ago. Some of the warming was due to a local (possibly temporary) effect via ocean currents from the Caribbean, which had also affected fish stocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Small_islands">Small islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Small islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alliance_of_Small_Island_States" title="Alliance of Small Island States">Alliance of Small Island States</a></div>
<p>In a literature assessment, Mimura <i>et al.</i> (2007:689)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> concluded that on small islands, <a href="/wiki/Subsistence_agriculture" title="Subsistence agriculture">subsistence</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_agriculture" title="Commercial agriculture">commercial agriculture</a> would very likely be adversely affected by climate change. This projection was assigned "high confidence."
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty_impacts">Poverty impacts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Poverty impacts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Development_Institute" title="Overseas Development Institute">Overseas Development Institute</a> (ODI) have investigated the potential impacts climate change could have on agriculture, and how this would affect attempts at alleviating poverty in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccandpoverty_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-ccandpoverty-77">[77]</a></sup> They argued that the effects from moderate climate change are likely to be mixed for developing countries. However, the vulnerability of the poor in developing countries to short term impacts from climate change, notably the increased frequency and severity of adverse weather events is likely to have a negative impact. This, they say, should be taken into account when defining <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">agricultural policy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ccandpoverty_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-ccandpoverty-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mitigation_and_adaptation_in_developing_countries">Mitigation and adaptation in developing countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Mitigation and adaptation in developing countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPCC" title="IPCC">IPCC</a>) has reported that agriculture is responsible for over a quarter of total global greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC-78">[78]</a></sup> Given that agriculture’s share in global <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> (GDP) is about 4%, these figures suggest that <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> is highly <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> intensive. Innovative agricultural practices and technologies can play a role in <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> change mitigation <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mitigation_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-mitigation-79">[79]</a></sup> and adaptation. This adaptation and mitigation potential is nowhere more pronounced than in developing countries where agricultural productivity remains low; poverty, vulnerability and food insecurity remain high; and the direct effects of climate change are expected to be especially harsh. Creating the necessary agricultural technologies and harnessing them to enable developing countries to adapt their agricultural systems to changing climate will require innovations in policy and institutions as well.  In this context, institutions and policies are important at multiple scales.
</p><p>Travis Lybbert and Daniel Sumner<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> suggest six policy principles:
(1) The best policy and institutional responses will enhance information flows, incentives and flexibility.
(2) Policies and institutions that promote economic development and reduce poverty will often improve agricultural adaptation and may also pave the way for more effective climate change mitigation through agriculture.
(3) Business as usual among the world’s poor is not adequate.
(4) Existing technology options must be made more available and accessible without overlooking complementary capacity and investments.
(5) Adaptation and mitigation in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> will require local responses, but effective policy responses must also reflect global impacts and inter-linkages.
(6) <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">Trade</a> will play a critical role in both mitigation and adaptation, but will itself be shaped importantly by climate change.
</p><p>The Agricultural Model Intercomparison and Improvement Project (AgMIP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> was developed in 2010 to evaluate agricultural models and intercompare their ability to predict climate impacts. In sub-Saharan Africa and South Asia, South America and East Asia, AgMIP regional research teams (RRTs) are conducting integrated assessments to improve understanding of agricultural impacts of climate change (including biophysical and economic impacts) at national and regional scales. Other AgMIP initiatives include global gridded modeling, data and information technology (IT) tool development, simulation of crop pests and diseases, site-based crop-climate sensitivity studies, and aggregation and scaling.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_development_models">Crop development models</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Crop development models">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Models for climate behavior are frequently inconclusive. In order to further study effects of global warming on agriculture, other types of models, such as <i>crop development models</i>, <i>yield prediction</i>, quantities of <i>water or fertilizer consumed</i>, can be used. Such models condense the knowledge accumulated of the climate, soil, and effects observed of the results of various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_practices" title="Agricultural practices">agricultural practices</a>. They thus could make it possible to test strategies of adaptation to modifications of the environment.
</p><p>Because these models are necessarily simplifying natural conditions (often based on the assumption that weeds, disease and insect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pest_(animal)" title="Pest (animal)">pests</a> are controlled), it is not clear whether the results they give will have an <i>in-field</i> reality. However, some results are partly validated with an increasing number of experimental results.
</p><p>Other models, such as <i>insect and disease development</i> models based on climate projections are also used (for example simulation of <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphid</a> reproduction or <a href="/wiki/Septoria" title="Septoria">septoria</a> (cereal fungal disease) development).
</p><p>Scenarios are used in order to estimate climate changes effects on crop development and yield. Each scenario is defined as a set of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meteorological" title="Meteorological">meteorological</a> variables, based on generally accepted projections. For example, many models are running simulations based on doubled <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> projections, temperatures raise ranging from 1 °C up to 5 °C, and with rainfall levels an increase or decrease of 20%. Other parameters may include <a href="/wiki/Humidity" title="Humidity">humidity</a>, wind, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_variation" title="Solar variation">solar activity</a>. Scenarios of crop models are testing farm-level adaptation, such as sowing date shift, climate adapted species (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernalisation" title="Vernalisation">vernalisation</a> need, heat and cold resistance), <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> and fertilizer adaptation, resistance to disease. Most developed models are about wheat, maize, rice and <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature_potential_effect_on_growing_period">Temperature potential effect on growing period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Temperature potential effect on growing period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
