<p>Duration of crop <a href="/wiki/Cell_growth" title="Cell growth">growth</a> <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">cycles</a> are above all, related to temperature. An increase in temperature will speed up development. In the case of an annual crop, the duration between <a href="/wiki/Sowing" title="Sowing">sowing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harvesting" title="Harvesting">harvesting</a> will shorten (for example, the duration in order to harvest corn could shorten between one and four weeks). The shortening of such a cycle could have an adverse effect on productivity because <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a> would occur sooner.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_of_elevated_carbon_dioxide_on_crops">Effect of elevated carbon dioxide on crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Effect of elevated carbon dioxide on crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> is essential to plant growth. Rising CO<sub>2</sub> concentration in the atmosphere can have both positive and negative consequences.
</p><p>Increased CO<sub>2</sub> is expected to have positive physiological effects by increasing the rate of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. This is known as 'carbon dioxide fertilisation'.  Currently, the amount of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere is 410 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a>. In comparison, the amount of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> is 210,000 ppm. This means that often plants may be starved of carbon dioxide as the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that fixes CO<sub>2</sub>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RuBisCo" title="RuBisCo">RuBisCo</a>, also fixes oxygen in the process of <a href="/wiki/Photorespiration" title="Photorespiration">photorespiration</a>. The effects of an increase in carbon dioxide would be higher on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C3_plants" title="C3 plants">C3 crops</a> (such as wheat) than on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C4_plants" title="C4 plants">C4 crops</a> (such as maize), because the former is more susceptible to carbon dioxide shortage. Studies have shown that increased CO<sub>2</sub> leads to fewer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a> developing on plants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> which leads to reduced water usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Under optimum conditions of temperature and humidity, the yield increase could reach 36%, if the levels of carbon dioxide are doubled.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A study in 2014 posited that CO<sub>2</sub> fertilisation is underestimated due to not explicitly representing CO<sub>2</sub> diffusion inside leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further, few studies have looked at the impact of elevated carbon dioxide concentrations on whole farming systems. Most models study the relationship between CO<sub>2</sub> and productivity in isolation from other factors associated with climate change, such as an increased frequency of <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">extreme weather events</a>, seasonal shifts, and so on.
</p><p>In 2005, the Royal Society in London concluded that the purported benefits of elevated carbon dioxide concentrations are "likely to be far lower than previously estimated when factors such as increasing <a href="/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone" title="Tropospheric ozone">ground-level ozone</a> are taken into account."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Myers et al. 2014, reported based on a meta study, that elevated CO<sub>2</sub> levels reduced the nutrient concentrations (zinc, iron, and less so protein) of plants, with C3 grasses and legumes specifically affected and C4 plants not so much.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_quality">Effect on quality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Effect on quality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the IPCC's TAR, "The importance of climate change impacts on grain and forage quality emerges from new research. For rice, the amylose content of the grain—a major determinant of cooking quality—is increased under elevated CO<sub>2</sub>" (Conroy et al., 1994). Cooked rice grain from plants grown in high-CO<sub>2</sub> environments would be firmer than that from today's plants. However, concentrations of iron and zinc, which are important for human nutrition, would be lower (Seneweera and Conroy, 1997). Moreover, the protein content of the grain decreases under combined increases of temperature and CO<sub>2</sub> (Ziska et al., 1997).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Studies using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-Air_Concentration_Enrichment" title="Free-Air Concentration Enrichment">FACE</a> have shown that increases in CO<sub>2</sub> lead to decreased concentrations of micronutrients in crop plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> including decreased B vitamins in rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhu2018_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu2018-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> This may have knock-on effects on other parts of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> as herbivores will need to eat more food to gain the same amount of protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies have shown that higher CO<sub>2</sub> levels lead to reduced plant uptake of nitrogen (and a smaller number showing the same for trace elements such as zinc) resulting in crops with lower nutritional value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Food,_the_Bad,_and_the_Ugly_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Food,_the_Bad,_and_the_Ugly-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> This would primarily impact on populations in poorer countries less able to compensate by eating more food, more varied diets, or possibly taking supplements.
</p><p>Reduced nitrogen content in grazing plants has also been shown to reduce animal productivity in sheep, which depend on microbes in their gut to digest plants, which in turn depend on nitrogen intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Food,_the_Bad,_and_the_Ugly_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Food,_the_Bad,_and_the_Ugly-92">[92]</a></sup>  Because of the lack of water available to crops in warmer countries they struggle to survive as they suffer from dehydration, taking into account the increasing demand for water outside of agriculture as well as other agricultural demands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_surfaces_and_climate_changes">Agricultural surfaces and climate changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Agricultural surfaces and climate changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Climate change may increase the amount of <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> in high-latitude region by reduction of the amount of frozen lands. A 2005 study reports that temperature in Siberia has increased three degree Celsius in average since 1960 (much more than the rest of the world).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> However, reports about the impact of global warming on Russian agriculture<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> indicate conflicting probable effects : while they expect a northward extension of farmable lands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> they also warn of possible productivity losses and increased risk of drought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Sea levels are expected to get up to one meter higher by 2100, though this projection is disputed. A rise in the sea level would result in an agricultural <a href="/wiki/Land_consumption" title="Land consumption">land loss</a>, in particular in areas such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_East_Asia" title="South East Asia">South East Asia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">Erosion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_level_rise" title="Sea level rise">submergence of shorelines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Water_table" title="Water table">water table</a> due to the increased sea levels, could mainly affect agriculture through <a href="/wiki/Inundation" title="Inundation">inundation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Depression_(geology)" title="Depression (geology)">low-lying lands</a>.
</p><p>Low-lying areas such as Bangladesh, India and Vietnam will experience major loss of rice crop if sea levels rise as expected by the end of the century. Vietnam for example relies heavily on its southern tip, where the Mekong Delta lies, for rice planting. Any rise in sea level of no more than a meter will drown several km<sup>2</sup> of rice paddies, rendering Vietnam incapable of producing its main staple and export of rice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Erosion_and_fertility">Erosion and fertility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Erosion and fertility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The warmer atmospheric temperatures observed over the past decades are expected to lead to a more vigorous hydrological cycle, including more extreme rainfall events.  <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">Erosion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" title="Soil degradation">soil degradation</a> is more likely to occur.  Soil <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertility</a> would also be affected by global warming. Increased erosion in agricultural landscapes from anthropogenic factors can occur with losses of up to 22% of soil carbon in 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>  However, because the ratio of soil organic carbon to nitrogen is mediated by soil biology such that it maintains a narrow range, a doubling of soil organic carbon is likely to imply a doubling in the storage of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> in soils as organic nitrogen, thus providing higher available nutrient levels for plants, supporting higher yield potential. The demand for imported fertilizer nitrogen could decrease, and provide the opportunity for changing costly <a href="/wiki/Fertilisation" title="Fertilisation">fertilisation</a> strategies.
</p><p>Due to the extremes of climate that would result, the increase in precipitations would probably result in greater risks of erosion, whilst at the same time providing soil with better hydration, according to the intensity of the rain. The possible evolution of the <a href="/wiki/Organic_matter" title="Organic matter">organic matter</a> in the soil is a highly contested issue: while the increase in the temperature would induce a greater rate in the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minerals" title="Minerals">minerals</a>, lessening the <a href="/wiki/Soil_organic_matter" title="Soil organic matter">soil organic matter</a> content, the atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> concentration would tend to increase it.
</p>
<h3><span id="Potential_effects_of_global_climate_change_on_pests.2C_diseases_and_weeds"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_effects_of_global_climate_change_on_pests,_diseases_and_weeds">Potential effects of global climate change on pests, diseases and weeds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Potential effects of global climate change on pests, diseases and weeds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A very important point to consider is that weeds would undergo the same acceleration of cycle as cultivated crops, and would also benefit from carbonaceous fertilization. Since most weeds are C3 plants, they are likely to compete even more than now against C4 crops such as corn. However, on the other hand, some results make it possible to think that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weedkiller" title="Weedkiller">weedkillers</a> could increase in effectiveness with the temperature increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Global warming would cause an increase in rainfall in some areas, which would lead to an increase of atmospheric humidity and the duration of the <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">wet seasons</a>. Combined with higher temperatures, these could favor the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungal</a> diseases. Similarly, because of higher temperatures and humidity, there could be an increased pressure from insects and <a href="/wiki/Vector_(epidemiology)" title="Vector (epidemiology)">disease vectors</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glacier_retreat_and_disappearance">Glacier retreat and disappearance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Glacier retreat and disappearance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The continued <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming#Glacier_retreat_and_disappearance" title="Effects of global warming">retreat of glaciers</a> will have a number of different quantitative impacts. In the areas that are heavily dependent on <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">water runoff</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a> that melt during the warmer summer months, a continuation of the current retreat will eventually deplete the glacial ice and substantially reduce or eliminate runoff. A reduction in runoff will affect the ability to <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigate</a> crops and will reduce summer stream flows necessary to keep dams and reservoirs replenished.
</p><p>Approximately 2.4 billion people live in the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> of the Himalayan rivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, China, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, Afghanistan, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, Nepal and <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> could experience floods followed by severe droughts in coming decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> alone, the Ganges provides water for drinking and farming for more than 500 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The west coast of North America, which gets much of its water from glaciers in mountain ranges such as the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevada</a>, also would be affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_and_UV-B">Ozone and UV-B</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Ozone and UV-B">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some scientists think agriculture could be affected by any decrease in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratospheric_ozone" title="Stratospheric ozone">stratospheric ozone</a>, which could increase biologically dangerous <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet radiation B</a>. Excess ultraviolet radiation B can directly affect plant <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a> and cause massive amounts of <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a>, and indirectly through changed <a href="/wiki/Pollinator" title="Pollinator">pollinator</a> behavior, though such changes are not simple to quantify.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> However, it has not yet been ascertained whether an increase in greenhouse gases would decrease stratospheric ozone levels.
</p><p>In addition, a possible effect of rising temperatures is significantly higher levels of <a href="/wiki/Tropospheric_ozone" title="Tropospheric ozone">ground-level ozone</a>, which would substantially lower yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="ENSO_effects_on_agriculture">ENSO effects on agriculture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: ENSO effects on agriculture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>ENSO (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o_Southern_Oscillation" title="El Niño Southern Oscillation">El Niño Southern Oscillation</a>) will affect monsoon patterns more intensely in the future as climate change warms up the ocean's water. Crops that lie on the equatorial belt or under the tropical Walker circulation, such as rice, will be affected by varying monsoon patterns and more unpredictable weather. Scheduled planting and harvesting based on weather patterns will become less effective.
</p><p>Areas such as Indonesia where the main crop consists of rice will be more vulnerable to the increased intensity of ENSO effects in the future of climate change. University of Washington professor, <a href="/wiki/David_Battisti" title="David Battisti">David Battisti</a>, researched the effects of future ENSO patterns on the Indonesian rice agriculture using [IPCC]'s 2007 annual report<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and 20 different logistical models mapping out climate factors such as wind pressure, sea-level, and humidity, and found that rice harvest will experience a decrease in yield. Bali and Java, which holds 55% of the rice yields in Indonesia, will be likely to experience 9–10% probably of delayed monsoon patterns, which prolongs the hungry season. Normal planting of rice crops begin in October and harevest by January. However, as climate change affects ENSO and consequently delays planting, harvesting will be late and in drier conditions, resulting in less potential yields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_agriculture_on_climate_change">Impact of agriculture on climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Impact of agriculture on climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture,_by_region,_1990-2010.png"><img alt="refer to caption and image description" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="1363" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_by_region%2C_1990-2010.png/450px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_by_region%2C_1990-2010.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_by_region%2C_1990-2010.png/675px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_by_region%2C_1990-2010.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_by_region%2C_1990-2010.png/900px-Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture%2C_by_region%2C_1990-2010.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture,_by_region,_1990-2010.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture, by region, 1990-2010.</div></div></div>
<p>The agricultural sector is a driving force in the gas emissions and land use effects thought to cause climate change. In addition to being a significant user of <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land</a> and consumer of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>, agriculture contributes directly to <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions through practices such as rice production and the raising of livestock;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> according to the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>, the three main causes of the increase in greenhouse gases observed over the past 250 years have been fossil fuels, land use, and agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Land use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Agriculture contributes to greenhouse gas increases through land use in four main ways:
</p>
<ul><li>CO<sub>2</sub> releases linked to <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a></li>
<li>Methane releases from <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice cultivation</a></li>
<li>Methane releases from <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a> in cattle</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">Nitrous oxide</a> releases from <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> application</li></ul>
<p>Together, these agricultural processes comprise 54% of <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a>, roughly 80% of nitrous oxide emissions, and virtually all carbon dioxide emissions tied to land use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The planet's major changes to <a href="/wiki/Land_cover" title="Land cover">land cover</a> since 1750 have resulted from <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_region" title="Temperate region">temperate regions</a>: when forests and woodlands are cleared to make room for fields and <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastures</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> of the affected area increases, which can result in either warming or cooling effects, depending on local conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Deforestation also affects regional <a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">carbon reuptake</a>, which can result in increased concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a>, the dominant greenhouse gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Land-clearing methods such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a> compound these effects by burning <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomatter" title="Biomatter">biomatter</a>, which directly releases greenhouse gases and particulate matter such as <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> into the air.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock">Livestock</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Livestock">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Livestock and livestock-related activities such as deforestation and increasingly fuel-intensive farming practices are responsible for over 18%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lls_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-lls-116">[116]</a></sup> of human-made greenhouse gas emissions, including:
</p>
<ul><li>9% of global <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> emissions</li>
<li>35–40% of global <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> emissions (chiefly due to <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a>)</li>
<li>64% of global nitrous oxide emissions (chiefly due to <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a> use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lls_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-lls-116">[116]</a></sup>)</li></ul>
<p>Livestock activities also contribute disproportionately to land-use effects, since crops such as <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a> are cultivated in order to feed the animals.
</p><p>In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Enteric_fermentation" title="Enteric fermentation">enteric fermentation</a> accounted for 43% of the total greenhouse gas emissions from all agricultural activity in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The meat from ruminants has a higher carbon equivalent footprint than other meats or vegetarian sources of protein based on a global meta-analysis of lifecycle assessment studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Methane production by animals, principally ruminants, is estimated 15-20% global production of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Worldwide, livestock production occupies 70% of all land used for agriculture, or 30% of the land surface of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lls_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-lls-116">[116]</a></sup> The way livestock is grazed also decides the fertility of the land in the future, not circulating grazing can lead to unhealthy soil and the expansion of livestock farms effects the habitats of local animals and has led to the drop in population of many local species from being displaced.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_agriculture&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 25em; -webkit-column-width: 25em; column-width: 25em; list-style-type: decimal;">
